A C++ wrapper naturally works in inference mode. OK, let me write the full code with a custom attention kernel. Here's the kernel design: **Custom Attention Kernel** I will write a kernel that: - Uses one block per (head, query_tile) - Each block has 128 threads - Each thread computes one query row's output - Uses shared memory for Q tile and K/V tiles - Implements causal masking - Uses online softmax Kernel grid: (num_query_tiles, num_heads * batch) = (ceil(1023/32)=32, 12) = (32, 12) Block size: 128 threads Each block processes 32 query rows (since 128 threads and ceil(32/128) = 1 row per thread, but we can have 4 threads per row for better parallelism). Actually, with 128 threads and 32 rows, I can have 4 threads per row. Each thread computes 64/4 = 16 elements of the dot product and output. This is more parallel and better utilizes the warp. Let me design it this way. For 4 threads per row: - Thread IDs 0-3 handle row 0, computing 16 dimensions each. - Thread IDs 4-7 handle row 1, computing 16 dimensions each. - ... - Thread IDs 124-127 handle row 31. In the C++ code: ```cuda int row_per_block = 32; int threads_per_row = blockDim.x / row_per_block; // 128/32 = 4 int row_local = threadIdx.x / threads_per_row; int dim_group = threadIdx.x % threads_per_row; ``` Wait, but 128 threads / 32 rows = 4 threads per row. If head_dim = 64, each thread handles 64/4 = 16 dimensions. This is perfect. But each thread computes partial dot products for 16 dimensions, then all 4 threads need to reduce their partial results. This requires warp-level reduction. ```cuda __shared__ float shared_scores[row_per_block]; // Not needed if we maintain per-thread ``` Actually, let me think about the dot product with 4 threads per row. Thread `t` in row `r` computes: ```cuda partial_score = 0; for d = dim_group * 16; d < (dim_group + 1) * 16; d++) { partial_score += Qs[r][d] * Ks[c][d]; } ``` Then all 4 threads in the row do a warp reduction on `partial_score`. After reduction, all 4 threads have the full score. This is essentially a warp-level parallel reduction. On H100, `__shfl_xor_sync` does this efficiently. ```cuda float score = partial_score; score += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, score, 1); score += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, score, 2); // After these, thread 0 of each group of 4 has the full score (for its lane) ``` Wait, `__shfl_xor_sync` works within a warp of 32 threads. In our case, 4 threads per row means threads within the same row are in the same warp. Let me check: Thread IDs: 0, 1, 2, 3 handle row 0. Thread IDs: 4, 5, 6, 7 handle row 1. ... Thread IDs: 124, 125, 126, 127 handle row 31. Are threads 0-3 in the same warp? Yes, a warp is 32 consecutive threads. So threads 0-31 are in the same warp. Threads 0-3 (row 0) and threads 4-7 (row 1) and threads 8-11 (row 2), etc., are all in warp 0. This is actually bad for shfl reduction! Threads 0-3 want to reduce among themselves, but they're interleaved with threads from other rows in the same warp. If I do `__shfl_xor_sync(mask, score, 1)`, it exchanges between threads differing by 1 bit. Thread 0 exchanges with thread 1, thread 2 with thread 3, etc. For thread 0 and thread 1, they're in the same row (row 0). Good. `__shfl_xor_sync(mask, score, 2)`: thread 0 exchanges with thread 2. Both are in row 0. Good. But what about the mask? I need to make sure only threads in the same row participate. Since all threads in a warp will execute the instruction (warp-level), I need threads from other rows to not interfere. If they return 0 or don't participate... actually, `__shfl_xor_sync` requires a mask of participating threads. If I use a mask of `0xFFFFFFFF` (all threads in warp), then thread 0 will also get values from threads 4, 8, etc. when doing `__shfl_xor_sync(mask, score, 4)`? No, `__shfl_xor_sync(val, laneMask)` XORs the lane ID with `laneMask`. For laneMask=4, thread 0 gets from thread 4, thread 1 gets from thread 5, etc. If I only do laneMask=1 and laneMask=2, for a 4-thread group: - Starting with [s0, s1, s2, s3, s4, s5, s6, s7, ...] - After xor(1): thread 0 has s0+s1, thread 2 has s2+s3, etc. - After xor(2): thread 0 has s0+s1+s2+s3, thread 4 has s4+s5+s6+s7, etc. Wait, this doesn't work the way I want. Let me trace it carefully. Initially: - lane 0: score_0 (row 0) - lane 1: score_1 (row 0) - lane 2: score_2 (row 0) - lane 3: score_3 (row 0) - lane 4: score_4 (row 1) - lane 5: score_5 (row 1) - lane 6: score_6 (row 1) - lane 7: score_7 (row 1) - ... After `__shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, score, 1)`: - lane 0: score_0 + score_1 - lane 1: score_1 + score_0 - lane 2: score_2 + score_3 - lane 3: score_3 + score_2 - lane 4: score_4 + score_5 - ... After `__shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, score, 2)`: - lane 0: previous(lane0) + previous(lane2) = (score_0 + score_1) + (score_2 + score_3) - lane 1: previous(lane1) + previous(lane3) = (score_1 + score_0) + (score_3 + score_2) - lane 2: previous(lane2) + previous(lane0) = same - lane 3: previous(lane3) + previous(lane1) = same So after xor(1) and xor(2), ALL threads in a group of 4 have the same sum! Perfect. Thread 0 has the sum for row 0, thread 4 has the sum for row 1, etc. So the warp-level reduction works fine within the larger warp. But wait, I also need to broadcast the result to all 4 threads in the group. After the two shfl operations, lanes 0-3 all have the sum for row 0. Similarly lanes 4-7 have the sum for row 1. So I can just use the value directly in all threads. Great! Similarly for the output accumulation: each thread computes a partial output for 16 dimensions. But the output is computed as `sum(p * V[c][d])`, and `p` is the same for all dimensions in a given `c`. So each thread can compute its 16 dimensions independently, no reduction needed! Wait, but `p = exp(score - m)` is computed from the full score. All threads in the row need `p`. After the score reduction, all threads have `score` (the sum). Then they all compute `p = exp(score - m)` locally. Since `p` is a scalar, each thread can compute it independently (same value across the row). Then for each dimension `d`: ```cuda o_dim[d] += p * Vs[c][d]; ``` This is done independently by each thread for its assigned dimensions. No need for reduction! So the per-row parallelization across 4 threads works perfectly for the output accumulation. Now for loading Q and K/V: - Qs is in shared memory: shape [row_per_block][head_dim]. With row_per_block=32, head_dim=64, Qs = 32*64*4 = 8KB. - Each thread loads its assigned dimensions for its assigned row: ```cuda for (int d = dim_group * 16; d < (dim_group + 1) * 16; d++) { Qs[row_local][d] = Q_global[...]; } ``` - But threads in the same row have consecutive `dim_group` values (0, 1, 2, 3). Their accesses to Q_global are interleaved: - Thread (row=0, dg=0): reads d=0..15 - Thread (row=0, dg=1): reads d=16..31 - etc. These are consecutive in memory but read by different threads, which is coalesced. For Ks and Vs: similar loading pattern when loading from global to shared. For the dot product: ```cuda for (int c = 0; c < Bc; c++) { float partial = 0.0f; for (int d = dim_group * 16; d < (dim_group + 1) * 16; d++) { partial += Qs[row_local][d] * Ks[c][d]; } // Reduce partial across 4 threads partial += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, 1); partial += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, 2); float score = partial; // Update softmax and output // ... for (int d = dim_group * 16; d < (dim_group + 1) * 16; d++) { o[d] += p * Vs[c][d]; } } ``` This looks correct and efficient! For online softmax: ```cuda float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o[16] = {0}; for (int kv_tile = 0; kv_tile < num_kv_tiles; kv_tile++) { // Load Ks and Vs for this tile... for (int c = 0; c < Bc && k_global < seq_len; c++) { int k_global = kv_tile * Bc + c; if (k_global > q_global) continue; float partial = 0; for (int d = dim_start; d < dim_end; d++) { partial += Qs[row_local][d] * Ks[c][d]; } partial += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, 1); partial += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, 2); float score = partial * scale; float m_new = fmaxf(m, score); float exp_prev = expf(m - m_new); float exp_curr = expf(score - m_new); l = l * exp_prev + exp_curr; for (int d = 0; d < 16; d++) { o[d] = o[d] * exp_prev + exp_curr * Vs[c][dim_start + d]; } m = m_new; } } ``` After all tiles, normalize: ```cuda for (int d = 0; d < 16; d++) { O_global[...] = o[d] / l; } ``` This is excellent! Let me write this out for the full kernel. Wait, there are 3 remaining issues: 1. For smaller `q_global`, some K/V tiles are fully masked. I should skip them at the tile level for efficiency. 2. For `q_global` in one block and `k_global` in a tile, if all K/V in a tile are > q_global, skip the tile. 3. `__shfl_xor_sync` with mask: since we're operating within a warp of 32 threads, but our groups are 4 threads, we need the correct mask. Actually, the mask 0xFFFFFFFF means all 32 threads in the warp participate. This is fine since the shfl operation only exchanges between threads differing by specified bits. If we only do `xor(1)` and `xor(2)`, threads in different groups of 4 don't interfere (they remain in their own group for these operations). But what if we have `xor(4)`? Thread 0 would get from thread 4, which is a different row. We only need `xor(1)` and `xor(2)` for groups of 4, so 0xFFFFFFFF is fine. Actually, for safety, I should use a mask of 0xF (0b1111) for threads 0-3, 0xF0 for threads 4-7, etc. But `__shfl_xor_sync` takes a 32-bit mask representing the participating threads in a warp. For groups of 4, the mask would be: For threads 0-3: `0xF` (first 4 bits) For threads 4-7: `0xF0` (next 4 bits) etc. But constructing this mask per thread requires: ```cuda int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int group_id = lane_id / 4; unsigned int mask = 0xF << (group_id * 4); ``` Then use `__shfl_xor_sync(mask, partial, 1)` and `__shfl_xor_sync(mask, partial, 2)`. This is safer and avoids relying on the fact that `xor(1)` and `xor(2)` don't cross group boundaries. Let me use this. Wait, actually `__shfl_xor_sync(mask, val, laneMask)` uses `mask` to determine which threads are participating. For threads in a warp, if a thread is not in the mask, it returns 0 or the original value depending on behavior. Actually, non-participating threads are masked out. So if thread 0 calls with mask 0xF, only threads 0-3 participate. Thread 4 does not participate. This is exactly what we want. But what about the other 28 threads in the warp? They would execute the instruction but with their own masks. Since all threads in a warp are executing together (SIMT), and each thread computes its own mask, this should work fine. OK, let me write the kernel. Actually, I realize this is getting extremely complex for a single kernel. With 8 turns and 24 tool calls, I need to balance complexity with correctness. The embedding lookup is a small part of GPT2 inference, especially at batch_size=1 and seq_len=1023. The hidden size for GPT2 is 768. Embedding lookup is just gathering 1023 vectors from 50k vocabulary. That's about 1023 * 768 = 786k float reads = 3MB. Compared to attention which is O(seq^2 * hidden) ~ 1023^2 * 768 = 800M operations. So the embedding is negligible.
